### Section 1.1 Nuclear Structure:

The atomic nucleus is a complex system that is composed of protons and neutrons. The study of nuclear structure is crucial for understanding the behavior of nuclei and their interactions with other particles. In this section, we will explore the basic concepts of nuclear structure, including the nuclear force and the nuclear shell model.

#### 1.1a Nuclear Size and Shape

The size and shape of the atomic nucleus are fundamental properties that determine its behavior. The size of a nucleus is typically measured in terms of its radius, which is the distance from the center of the nucleus to its surface. The shape of a nucleus, on the other hand, refers to its spatial distribution of charge and mass.

The size and shape of a nucleus are determined by the balance between the nuclear force and the Coulomb force. The nuclear force is a strong, short-range force that holds the nucleons (protons and neutrons) together. The Coulomb force, on the other hand, is a weak, long-range force that acts between charged particles.

The nuclear force is responsible for the compact size of the nucleus. It is a repulsive force between protons and a attractive force between neutrons. This force is strong enough to overcome the Coulomb force between protons, which would otherwise cause them to repel each other. As a result, the nucleons are forced to cluster together, resulting in a small and dense nucleus.

The shape of a nucleus, on the other hand, is determined by the balance between the nuclear force and the Coulomb force. The nuclear force is responsible for the spherical shape of most nuclei. However, in some cases, the Coulomb force can overcome the nuclear force, resulting in a deformed nucleus. This is often seen in nuclei with an imbalance of protons and neutrons, known as isotopes.

The size and shape of a nucleus can also be affected by the presence of unstable nuclei. Unstable nuclei are prone to undergo nuclear fission, a process in which the nucleus splits into two or more smaller nuclei. This process can result in a change in the size and shape of the nucleus, as well as the release of energy in the form of radiation.

In summary, the size and shape of a nucleus are determined by the balance between the nuclear force and the Coulomb force. The nuclear force is responsible for the compact size and spherical shape of most nuclei, while the Coulomb force can result in deformed nuclei and nuclear fission. Understanding the size and shape of nuclei is crucial for understanding their behavior and interactions with other particles.

# Nuclear shell model

### Other properties of nuclei

This model also predicts or explains with some success other properties of nuclei, in particular spin and parity of nuclei ground states, and to some extent their excited nuclear states as well. Take <chem|17|8|O> (oxygen-17) as an example: Its nucleus has eight protons filling the three first proton "shells", eight neutrons filling the three first neutron "shells", and one extra neutron. All protons in a complete proton shell have zero total angular momentum, since their angular momenta cancel each other. The same is true for neutrons. All protons in the same level ("n") have the same parity (either +1 or ‚àí1), and since the parity of a pair of particles is the product of their parities, an even number of protons from the same level ("n") will have +1 parity. Thus the total angular momentum of the eight protons and the first eight neutrons is zero, and their total parity is +1. This means that the spin (i.e. angular momentum) of the nucleus, as well as its parity, are fully determined by that of the ninth neutron. This one is in the first (i.e. lowest energy) state of the 4th shell, which is a d-shell ("‚Ñì" = 2), and since "p" = (‚àí1)<sup|"‚Ñì">, this gives the nucleus an overall parity of¬†+1. This 4th d-shell has a "j" = <frac|5|2>, thus the nucleus of <chem|17|8|O> is expected to have positive parity and total angular momentum <frac|5|2>, which indeed it has.

The rules for the ordering of the nucleus shells are similar to Hund's Rules of the atomic shells, however, unlike its use in atomic physics the completion of a shell is not signified by reaching the next "n", as such the shell model cannot accurately predict the order of excited nuclei states, though it is very successful in predicting the ground states. The order of the first few terms are listed as follows: 1s, 1p<frac|3|2>, 1p<frac|1|2>, 1d<frac|5|2>, 2s, 1d<frac|3|2>... For further clarification on the notation refer to the article on the Russell<en dash

### Section 1.1 Nuclear Structure:

The atomic nucleus is a complex system that is composed of protons and neutrons. The study of nuclear structure is crucial for understanding the behavior of nuclei and their interactions with other particles. In this section, we will explore the basic concepts of nuclear structure, including the nuclear force and the nuclear shell model.

#### 1.1a Nuclear Size and Shape

The size and shape of the atomic nucleus are fundamental properties that determine its behavior. The size of a nucleus is typically measured in terms of its radius, which is the distance from the center of the nucleus to its surface. The shape of a nucleus, on the other hand, refers to its spatial distribution of charge and mass.

The size and shape of a nucleus are determined by the balance between the nuclear force and the Coulomb force. The nuclear force is a strong, short-range force that holds the nucleons (protons and neutrons) together. The Coulomb force, on the other hand, is a weak, long-range force that acts between charged particles.

The nuclear force is responsible for the compact size of the nucleus. It is a repulsive force between protons and a attractive force between neutrons. This force is strong enough to overcome the Coulomb force between protons, which would otherwise cause them to repel each other. As a result, the nucleons are forced to cluster together, resulting in a small and dense nucleus.

The shape of a nucleus, on the other hand, is determined by the balance between the nuclear force and the Coulomb force. The nuclear force is responsible for the spherical shape of most nuclei. However, in some cases, the Coulomb force can overcome the nuclear force, resulting in a deformed nucleus. This is often seen in nuclei with an imbalance of protons and neutrons, known as isotopes.

The size and shape of a nucleus can also be affected by the presence of unstable nuclei. Unstable nuclei are prone to undergo nuclear fission, a process in which the nucleus splits into two or more smaller nuclei. This process can result in a change in the size and shape of the nucleus, as well as the distribution of charge and mass.

### Subsection 1.1b Nuclear Shell Model

The nuclear shell model is a theoretical model used to describe the structure of atomic nuclei. It is based on the concept of energy levels, similar to the electron shells in atoms. In the nuclear shell model, the energy levels are determined by the number of nucleons in the nucleus.

The model predicts that as the number of nucleons in a nucleus increases, the energy levels also increase. This is due to the Pauli exclusion principle, which states that no two nucleons can occupy the same energy level. As a result, the nucleons are forced to occupy higher energy levels, leading to an increase in the overall energy of the nucleus.

The nuclear shell model also predicts the existence of closed shells, similar to the closed electron shells in atoms. These closed shells are characterized by a stable configuration of nucleons, with all the energy levels filled. This results in a lower overall energy for the nucleus, making it more stable.

The nuclear shell model has been successful in predicting the properties of nuclei, including their spin and parity. It has also been used to explain the stability of certain nuclei, known as magic numbers, which have closed shells of nucleons.

In conclusion, the nuclear shell model is a powerful tool for understanding the structure and behavior of atomic nuclei. Its predictions have been confirmed through various experiments and have greatly contributed to our understanding of nuclear physics.

### Section: 1.1 Nuclear Structure

The atomic nucleus is a complex system that is responsible for the stability and properties of atoms. It is composed of protons and neutrons, collectively known as nucleons, which are held together by the strong nuclear force. The study of nuclear structure is crucial for understanding the behavior of matter at the atomic level and has led to many technological advancements.

#### 1.1a Nuclear Models

There are several models that have been developed to describe the structure of the atomic nucleus. These models are based on different assumptions and provide different insights into the behavior of nuclei. In this section, we will discuss some of the most important nuclear models.

##### The Liquid Drop Model

The liquid drop model is one of the oldest and most intuitive models of nuclear structure. It compares the nucleus to a droplet of liquid, with the surface tension of the droplet representing the repulsive force between nucleons. The model also takes into account the Coulomb repulsion between protons and the attractive force between nucleons due to the strong nuclear force.

The liquid drop model is useful for understanding the general trends in nuclear stability. For example, it predicts that heavier nuclei are more stable than lighter ones, as the surface tension and Coulomb repulsion become more significant with increasing nuclear size. However, the model is not accurate for predicting the exact stability of specific nuclei.

##### The Shell Model

The shell model is a more sophisticated model of nuclear structure that takes into account the quantum nature of nucleons. It assumes that nucleons occupy specific energy levels, or shells, within the nucleus. The model is based on the concept of the Fermi gas, where nucleons behave as fermions and fill up the available energy levels in a similar way to electrons in an atom.

The shell model is particularly useful for explaining the stability of certain nuclei, known as magic numbers, where the nucleus has a closed shell configuration. These nuclei are exceptionally stable and have been observed to have unique properties, such as increased resistance to fission.

##### The Mean Field Model

The mean field model is a more recent development in nuclear structure theory. It combines elements of both the liquid drop model and the shell model. The model assumes that nucleons interact with an average field created by all the other nucleons, rather than directly with each other. This allows for a more accurate description of the nuclear potential energy.

The mean field model has been successful in predicting the stability of nuclei and has also been used to explain the behavior of nuclei in heavy ion collisions. It has also been extended to include the effects of correlations between nucleons, known as the correlated basis function method.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the mathematical formalism of nuclear structure and explore the equations that govern the behavior of nuclei.

#### 1.1b Nuclear Forces

The nuclear forces are the forces that hold the nucleus of an atom together. These forces are primarily due to the strong nuclear force, which is responsible for the binding of nucleons (protons and neutrons) within the nucleus. The strong nuclear force is one of the four fundamental forces in nature, along with the electromagnetic force, the weak nuclear force, and gravity.

The strong nuclear force is a short-range force, acting only over distances of about 10^-15 meters. This is much smaller than the size of a nucleus, which is typically on the order of 10^-14 meters. This short range is what allows the nucleus to be considered as a point-like object.

The strong nuclear force is responsible for the stability of the nucleus. It is the force that counteracts the Coulomb repulsion between protons and the surface tension of the nucleus, as described by the liquid drop model. The strong nuclear force is also responsible for the shell structure of the nucleus, as described by the shell model.

The strong nuclear force is mediated by the exchange of gluons, which are the particles of the strong nuclear force. Gluons are massless and have no electric charge, unlike the photons of the electromagnetic force. This allows the strong nuclear force to be much stronger than the electromagnetic force, as the Coulomb force decreases with distance, while the strong nuclear force remains constant.

In addition to the strong nuclear force, there are also other forces at play in the nucleus. The Coulomb repulsion between protons is responsible for the repulsion between positively charged particles in the nucleus. The surface tension of the nucleus, due to the repulsion between nucleons, is also important in determining the stability of the nucleus.

The nuclear forces play a crucial role in nuclear structure and are responsible for the stability and properties of atoms. Understanding these forces is essential for understanding the behavior of matter at the atomic level and has led to many technological advancements. In the next section, we will explore the concept of nuclear isomers, which are excited states of the nucleus that can have unique properties due to the nuclear forces.

#### 1.1c Nuclear Energy Levels

The energy levels of the nucleus are a crucial aspect of nuclear structure. They are determined by the quantum numbers of the nucleons within the nucleus, as well as the interactions between them. These energy levels can be visualized as the different energy states that a nucleus can occupy, similar to the energy levels of an atom.

The energy levels of the nucleus are quantized, meaning that they can only take on certain discrete values. This is a direct consequence of the wave-like nature of particles, as described by quantum mechanics. The energy levels of the nucleus can be calculated using the Schr√∂dinger equation, which describes the wave-like behavior of particles.

The lowest energy level of the nucleus is known as the ground state. This is the state that the nucleus occupies at absolute zero temperature. The ground state is characterized by the lowest possible energy of the nucleus. Any excitation of the nucleus from the ground state results in an increase in energy, leading to a higher energy level.

Higher energy levels of the nucleus are known as excited states. These states are characterized by higher energy than the ground state. Excited states can be reached through various processes, such as the absorption of a photon or the interaction with another nucleus.

The energy levels of the nucleus can also be affected by the nuclear forces. The strong nuclear force, for example, can cause a shift in the energy levels of the nucleus. This is known as the hyperfine structure of the nucleus. The hyperfine structure is a small shift in the energy levels of the nucleus due to the interactions between the nucleons.

The energy levels of the nucleus can also be affected by the presence of unstable nuclei. These nuclei, known as isomers, can exist in excited states for a certain period of time before decaying back to the ground state. This decay process is accompanied by the emission of radiation, which can be used to study the energy levels of the nucleus.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of nuclear isomers in more detail and discuss their role in nuclear structure.

#### 1.1d Nuclear Isomers

Nuclear isomers are excited states of the nucleus that can exist for a certain period of time before decaying back to the ground state. This decay process is accompanied by the emission of radiation, which can be used to study the energy levels of the nucleus. Nuclear isomers are particularly important in nuclear structure as they can provide insights into the behavior of nuclei under extreme conditions.

The concept of nuclear isomers was first proposed by George Gamow in the 1920s. He suggested that the nucleus of an atom could exist in excited states, similar to the excited states of an atom. However, it was not until the 1930s that the first nuclear isomer was observed. This was the isomer of uranium-238, which was discovered by Otto Hahn and Fritz Strassmann.

Nuclear isomers are characterized by their long half-lives, which can range from a few nanoseconds to several minutes. This is in contrast to the extremely short half-lives of most radioactive nuclei, which typically decay within a fraction of a second. The long half-lives of nuclear isomers allow for the study of the nucleus under conditions that are not possible with other radioactive nuclei.

The energy levels of nuclear isomers can be studied using techniques such as nuclear spectroscopy. This involves the absorption or emission of radiation by the nucleus, which can be measured to determine the energy levels of the nucleus. The energy levels of nuclear isomers can also be calculated using the Schr√∂dinger equation, similar to the energy levels of the ground state.

Nuclear isomers have been observed in a variety of nuclei, including those of uranium, neptunium, and plutonium. These nuclei are particularly prone to the formation of nuclear isomers due to their high neutron-to-proton ratios. The presence of nuclear isomers in these nuclei has been linked to the phenomenon of nuclear fission, which is responsible for the release of energy in nuclear reactions.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of nuclear fission in more detail and discuss its role in nuclear structure.

#### 1.1e Nuclear Reactions

Nuclear reactions are processes that involve the transformation of one or more atomic nuclei into different nuclei. These reactions can be spontaneous or induced, and they can result in the formation of new elements or the release of energy. Nuclear reactions are fundamental to many areas of science and technology, including nuclear energy, nuclear medicine, and nuclear weapons.

There are several types of nuclear reactions, including nuclear fission, nuclear fusion, and nuclear transmutation. Each of these reactions is governed by the principles of nuclear structure and the laws of quantum mechanics.

##### Nuclear Fission

Nuclear fission is a type of nuclear reaction in which a heavy nucleus splits into two or more lighter nuclei. This process is typically accompanied by the release of a large amount of energy, which can be harnessed for various applications. The most common type of nuclear fission is the fission of uranium-235, which is used in nuclear power plants.

The fission of uranium-235 can be represented by the following equation:

$$
^{235}_{92}U + ^1_0n \rightarrow ^{141}_{56}Ba + ^{92}_{36}Kr + 3^1_0n + \text{Energy}
$$

In this equation, uranium-235 is bombarded with a neutron, resulting in the splitting of the nucleus into barium-141, krypton-92, and three additional neutrons. This process releases a large amount of energy, which can be used to generate electricity.

##### Nuclear Fusion

Nuclear fusion is a type of nuclear reaction in which two light nuclei combine to form a heavier nucleus. This process is typically accompanied by the release of a large amount of energy, which can be used for various applications. The most common type of nuclear fusion is the fusion of hydrogen nuclei, which is used in hydrogen bombs.

The fusion of hydrogen nuclei can be represented by the following equation:

$$
^1_1H + ^1_1H \rightarrow ^2_1D + \text{Energy}
$$

In this equation, two hydrogen nuclei combine to form a deuteron, which is a nucleus of deuterium. This process releases a large amount of energy, which can be used for various applications.

##### Nuclear Transmutation

Nuclear transmutation is a type of nuclear reaction in which a nucleus is transformed into a different nucleus. This process can be induced by bombarding the nucleus with particles or radiation. Nuclear transmutation is used in nuclear medicine for the treatment of certain diseases.

The transmutation of a nucleus can be represented by the following equation:

$$
^{A}_{Z}X + ^1_0n \rightarrow ^{A}_{Z+1}Y + \text{Energy}
$$

In this equation, a nucleus of element X is bombarded with a neutron, resulting in the transformation of the nucleus into a nucleus of element Y. This process releases a large amount of energy, which can be used for various applications.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of nuclear energy in more detail and discuss its role in nuclear structure.

#### 1.1f Nuclear Stability

Nuclear stability refers to the ability of a nucleus to resist changes in its structure. This is a crucial aspect of nuclear structure, as it determines the likelihood of a nucleus undergoing nuclear fission, nuclear fusion, or other nuclear reactions. The concept of nuclear stability is closely related to the concept of nuclear binding energy, which is the energy required to break a nucleus into its individual nucleons.

The stability of a nucleus can be determined by comparing its binding energy with the binding energies of other nuclei. If a nucleus has a higher binding energy than other nuclei, it is considered to be more stable. This is because a more stable nucleus requires more energy to break it apart, making it less likely to undergo nuclear reactions.

The stability of a nucleus can also be determined by examining its nuclear potential energy. The nuclear potential energy is the energy required to create a nucleus from its individual nucleons. If a nucleus has a lower nuclear potential energy than other nuclei, it is considered to be more stable. This is because a more stable nucleus requires less energy to create, making it less likely to undergo nuclear reactions.

The stability of a nucleus can be further understood by examining its nuclear potential energy curve. This curve shows the potential energy of a nucleus as a function of its size. The minimum point on this curve represents the most stable nucleus size. Nuclei that are smaller than this size are considered to be unstable, while nuclei that are larger than this size are considered to be superstable.

The stability of a nucleus can also be affected by the presence of nuclear isomers. These are excited states of a nucleus that have longer half-lives than normal excited states. The presence of nuclear isomers can increase the stability of a nucleus by providing an additional energy barrier that must be overcome for nuclear reactions to occur.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of nuclear energy in more detail and discuss its role in nuclear structure.

#### 1.1g Nuclear Decay

Nuclear decay is a process in which an unstable atomic nucleus loses energy by emitting radiation in the form of alpha particles, beta particles, or gamma rays. This process is a spontaneous form of radioactive decay and is a common method of nuclear energy loss. The energy loss during nuclear decay is accompanied by a decrease in the atomic mass number (A) and the atomic number (Z).

The general equation for nuclear decay can be represented as:

$$
^{A}_{Z}X \rightarrow ^{A-4}_{Z-2}Be + ^{4}_{2}He + \text{Energy}
$$

In this equation, the initial nucleus (^{A}_{Z}X) decays into beryllium-9 (^{A-4}_{Z-2}Be) and helium-4 (^{4}_{2}He), with the release of energy. This process is known as alpha decay.

Nuclear decay can also occur through beta minus (Œ≤<sup>-</sup>) and beta plus (Œ≤<sup>+</sup>) processes. In these processes, the initial nucleus (^{A}_{Z}X) decays into a new nucleus (^{A}_{Z'}X') with a change in the atomic number (Z). This process is accompanied by the emission of an electron (Œ≤<sup>-</sup>) or a positron (Œ≤<sup>+</sup>) and an electron antineutrino (ŒΩ<sub>e</sub>) or an electron neutrino (ŒΩ<sub>e</sub>), respectively. The general equation for these processes can be represented as:

$$
^{A}_{Z}X \rightarrow ^{A}_{Z'}X' + e^{-} + \overline{\nu}_{e} \text{ (for Œ≤}^{-})
$$

$$
^{A}_{Z}X \rightarrow ^{A}_{Z'}X' + e^{+} + \nu_{e} \text{ (for Œ≤}^{+})
$$

In these equations, the initial nucleus (^{A}_{Z}X) decays into a new nucleus (^{A}_{Z'}X') with the emission of an electron (e^{-}) or a positron (e^{+}), and an electron antineutrino (ŒΩ<sub>e</sub>) or an electron neutrino (ŒΩ<sub>e</sub>), respectively.

Nuclear decay is a crucial aspect of nuclear structure, as it determines the stability of a nucleus. The stability of a nucleus can be determined by comparing its binding energy with the binding energies of other nuclei. If a nucleus has a higher binding energy than other nuclei, it is considered to be more stable. This is because a more stable nucleus requires more energy to break it apart, making it less likely to undergo nuclear reactions.

The stability of a nucleus can also be determined by examining its nuclear potential energy. The nuclear potential energy is the energy required to create a nucleus from its individual nucleons. If a nucleus has a lower nuclear potential energy than other nuclei, it is considered to be more stable. This is because a more stable nucleus requires less energy to create, making it less likely to undergo nuclear reactions.

The stability of a nucleus can be further understood by examining its nuclear potential energy curve. This curve shows the potential energy of a nucleus as a function of its size. The minimum point on this curve represents the most stable nucleus size. Nuclei that are smaller than this size are considered to be unstable, while nuclei that are larger than this size are considered to be superstable.

The stability of a nucleus can also be affected by the presence of nuclear isomers. These are excited states of a nucleus that have longer half-lives than normal excited states. The presence of nuclear isomers can increase the stability of a nucleus by providing an additional energy barrier that must be overcome for nuclear reactions to occur.

#### 1.1h Nuclear Fission

Nuclear fission is a type of nuclear reaction in which a heavy nucleus splits into two or more lighter nuclei. This process is typically accompanied by the release of a large amount of energy, which can be harnessed for various applications. The most common type of nuclear fission is the fission of uranium-235, which is used in nuclear power plants.

The general equation for nuclear fission can be represented as:

$$
^{A}_{Z}X + ^1_0n \rightarrow ^{A'}_{Z'}X' + ^{B}_{C}Y + \text{Energy}
$$

In this equation, the initial nucleus (^{A}_{Z}X) absorbs a neutron (^1_0n) and splits into a new nucleus (^{A'}_{Z'}X') and another nucleus (^{B}_{C}Y), with the release of energy. This process is known as induced fission.

Nuclear fission can also occur spontaneously, without the absorption of a neutron. This is known as spontaneous fission and is a common method of nuclear energy loss. The general equation for spontaneous fission can be represented as:

$$
^{A}_{Z}X \rightarrow ^{A'}_{Z'}X' + ^{B}_{C}Y + \text{Energy}
$$

In these equations, the initial nucleus (^{A}_{Z}X) splits into two or more new nuclei (^{A'}_{Z'}X' and ^{B}_{C}Y), with the release of energy.

Nuclear fission is a crucial aspect of nuclear structure, as it determines the stability of a nucleus. The stability of a nucleus can be determined by comparing its binding energy with the binding energies of other nuclei. If a nucleus has a higher binding energy than other nuclei, it is considered to be more stable. This is because a more stable nucleus requires more energy to break it apart, making it less likely to undergo nuclear reactions.

The stability of a nucleus can also be determined by examining its nuclear potential energy. The nuclear potential energy is the energy required to create a nucleus from its individual nucleons. If a nucleus has a lower nuclear potential energy than other nuclei, it is considered to be more stable. This is because a more stable nucleus requires less energy to create, making it less likely to undergo nuclear reactions.

The stability of a nucleus can be further understood by examining its nuclear potential energy curve. This curve shows the potential energy of a nucleus as a function of its size. The minimum point on this curve represents the most stable nucleus size. Nuclei that are smaller than this size are considered to be unstable, while nuclei that are larger than this size are considered to be superstable.

The stability of a nucleus can also be affected by the presence of nuclear isomers. These are excited states of a nucleus that have longer half-lives than normal excited states. The presence of nuclear isomers can increase the stability of a nucleus by providing an additional energy barrier that must be overcome for nuclear reactions to occur.

#### 1.1i Nuclear Fusion

Nuclear fusion is a type of nuclear reaction in which two light nuclei combine to form a heavier nucleus. This process is typically accompanied by the release of a large amount of energy, which can be harnessed for various applications. The most common type of nuclear fusion is the fusion of hydrogen nuclei, which is used in hydrogen bombs.

The general equation for nuclear fusion can be represented as:

$$
^{A}_{Z}X + ^{A}_{Z}X \rightarrow ^{A'}_{Z'}X' + \text{Energy}
$$

In this equation, two initial nuclei (^{A}_{Z}X) combine to form a new nucleus (^{A'}_{Z'}X'), with the release of energy. This process is known as symmetric fusion.

Nuclear fusion can also occur asymmetrically, where one nucleus is significantly heavier than the other. This is known as asymmetric fusion and is a common method of nuclear energy loss. The general equation for asymmetric fusion can be represented as:

$$
^{A}_{Z}X + ^{A'}_{Z'}X' \rightarrow ^{A'}_{Z'}X' + ^{B}_{C}Y + \text{Energy}
$$

In these equations, two initial nuclei (^{A}_{Z}X and ^{A'}_{Z'}X') combine to form a new nucleus (^{A'}_{Z'}X') and another nucleus (^{B}_{C}Y), with the release of energy.

Nuclear fusion is a crucial aspect of nuclear structure, as it determines the stability of a nucleus. The stability of a nucleus can be determined by comparing its binding energy with the binding energies of other nuclei. If a nucleus has a higher binding energy than other nuclei, it is considered to be more stable. This is because a more stable nucleus requires more energy to break it apart, making it less likely to undergo nuclear reactions.

The stability of a nucleus can also be determined by examining its nuclear potential energy. The nuclear potential energy is the energy required to create a nucleus from its individual nucleons. If a nucleus has a lower nuclear potential energy than other nuclei, it is considered to be more stable. This is because a more stable nucleus requires less energy to create, making it less likely to undergo nuclear reactions.

The stability of a nucleus can be further understood by examining its nuclear potential energy curve. This curve shows the potential energy of a nucleus as a function of its size. The minimum point on this curve represents the most stable nucleus size. Nuclei that are smaller than this size are considered to be unstable, while nuclei that are larger than this size are considered to be superstable.

The stability of a nucleus can also be affected by the presence of nuclear isomers. These are excited states of a nucleus that have longer half-lives than normal excited states. The presence of nuclear isomers can increase the stability of a nucleus by providing an additional energy barrier that must be overcome for nuclear reactions to occur.

#### 1.1j Nuclear Transmutation

Nuclear transmutation is a type of nuclear reaction in which a nucleus is transformed into a different nucleus. This process can occur through various methods, including nuclear fission, nuclear fusion, and nuclear capture. The most common type of nuclear transmutation is the transmutation of uranium-238 into plutonium-239, which is used in nuclear power plants.

The general equation for nuclear transmutation can be represented as:

$$
^{A}_{Z}X \rightarrow ^{A'}_{Z'}X' + \text{Energy}
$$

In this equation, an initial nucleus (^{A}_{Z}X) is transformed into a new nucleus (^{A'}_{Z'}X'), with the release of energy. This process is known as nuclear capture.

Nuclear transmutation can also occur through nuclear fission or nuclear fusion, as discussed in the previous sections. The general equations for these processes are:

$$
^{A}_{Z}X + ^1_0n \rightarrow ^{A'}_{Z'}X' + ^{B}_{C}Y + \text{Energy}
$$

$$
^{A}_{Z}X + ^{A}_{Z}X \rightarrow ^{A'}_{Z'}X' + \text{Energy}
$$

In these equations, an initial nucleus (^{A}_{Z}X) absorbs a neutron (^1_0n) or combines with another nucleus (^{A}_{Z}X) to form a new nucleus (^{A'}_{Z'}X'), with the release of energy.

Nuclear transmutation is a crucial aspect of nuclear structure, as it determines the stability of a nucleus. The stability of a nucleus can be determined by comparing its binding energy with the binding energies of other nuclei. If a nucleus has a higher binding energy than other nuclei, it is considered to be more stable. This is because a more stable nucleus requires more energy to break it apart, making it less likely to undergo nuclear reactions.

The stability of a nucleus can also be determined by examining its nuclear potential energy. The nuclear potential energy is the energy required to create a nucleus from its individual nucleons. If a nucleus has a lower nuclear potential energy than other nuclei, it is considered to be more stable. This is because a more stable nucleus requires less energy to create, making it less likely to undergo nuclear reactions.

The stability of a nucleus can be further understood by examining its nuclear potential energy curve. This curve shows the potential energy of a nucleus as a function of its size. The minimum point on this curve represents the most stable nucleus size. Nuclei that are smaller than this size are considered to be unstable, while nuclei that are larger than this size are considered to be superstable.

The stability of a nucleus can also be affected by the presence of nuclear isomers. These are excited states of a nucleus that have longer half-lives than normal excited states. The presence of nuclear isomers can increase the stability of a nucleus by providing an additional energy barrier that must be overcome for nuclear reactions to occur.

#### 1.1k Nuclear Energy Levels

Nuclear energy levels refer to the different energy states that a nucleus can occupy. These energy levels are quantized, meaning that a nucleus can only have certain discrete energy values. The energy levels of a nucleus can be represented by a potential energy curve, where the energy levels are represented by the minimum points of the curve.

The potential energy curve of a nucleus is a plot of the nuclear potential energy as a function of the size of the nucleus. The potential energy is the energy required to create the nucleus from its individual nucleons. The minimum points of the curve represent the most stable energy states of the nucleus. Nuclei that are smaller than these minimum points are considered to be unstable, while nuclei that are larger than these points are considered to be superstable.

The energy levels of a nucleus can be determined by examining its nuclear potential energy curve. The energy levels are represented by the minimum points of the curve, which are the points at which the curve is flattest. These points represent the most stable energy states of the nucleus. The energy difference between these points is the binding energy of the nucleus.

The binding energy of a nucleus is the energy required to break the nucleus apart into its individual nucleons. If a nucleus has a higher binding energy than other nuclei, it is considered to be more stable. This is because a more stable nucleus requires more energy to break it apart, making it less likely to undergo nuclear reactions.

The binding energy of a nucleus can be calculated using the following equation:

$$
\Delta E = E_{total} - \sum E_{individual}
$$

where $\Delta E$ is the binding energy, $E_{total}$ is the total energy of the nucleus, and $\sum E_{individual}$ is the sum of the individual energy levels of the nucleons in the nucleus.

The binding energy of a nucleus can also be determined by examining its nuclear potential energy curve. The binding energy is the energy difference between the minimum points of the curve. This energy difference represents the energy required to break the nucleus apart into its individual nucleons.

The binding energy of a nucleus can be further understood by examining its nuclear potential energy curve. The binding energy is the energy difference between the minimum points of the curve. This energy difference represents the energy required to break the nucleus apart into its individual nucleons. The larger the binding energy, the more stable the nucleus is considered to be.

#### 1.1l Nuclear Reactions

Nuclear reactions are processes that involve changes in the number of protons or neutrons in a nucleus. These reactions can be spontaneous or induced, and they can result in the formation of new elements or the release of energy. The study of nuclear reactions is a crucial aspect of nuclear physics, as it provides insights into the fundamental nature of matter and the forces that govern it.

There are several types of nuclear reactions, including nuclear fission, nuclear fusion, and nuclear transmutation. Each of these reactions has its own unique characteristics and implications.

Nuclear fission is a type of nuclear reaction in which a heavy nucleus splits into two or more lighter nuclei. This process is typically accompanied by the release of a large amount of energy, which can be harnessed for various applications. The most common type of nuclear fission is the fission of uranium-235, which is used in nuclear power plants.

The general equation for nuclear fission can be represented as:

$$
^{A}_{Z}X + ^1_0n \rightarrow ^{A'}_{Z'}X' + ^{B}_{C}Y + \text{Energy}
$$

In this equation, an initial nucleus (^{A}_{Z}X) absorbs a neutron (^1_0n) and splits into two or more new nuclei (^{A'}_{Z'}X' and ^{B}_{C}Y), with the release of energy.

Nuclear fusion is another type of nuclear reaction in which two light nuclei combine to form a heavier nucleus. This process is also typically accompanied by the release of a large amount of energy, which can be harnessed for various applications. The most common type of nuclear fusion is the fusion of hydrogen nuclei, which is used in hydrogen bombs.

The general equation for nuclear fusion can be represented as:

$$
^{A}_{Z}X + ^{A}_{Z}X \rightarrow ^{A'}_{Z'}X' + \text{Energy}
$$

In this equation, two initial nuclei (^{A}_{Z}X) combine to form a new nucleus (^{A'}_{Z'}X'), with the release of energy.

Nuclear transmutation is a type of nuclear reaction in which a nucleus is transformed into a different nucleus. This process can occur through various methods, including nuclear fission, nuclear fusion, and nuclear capture. The most common type of nuclear transmutation is the transmutation of uranium-238 into plutonium-239, which is used in nuclear power plants.

The general equation for nuclear transmutation can be represented as:

$$
^{A}_{Z}X \rightarrow ^{A'}_{Z'}X' + \text{Energy}
$$

In this equation, an initial nucleus (^{A}_{Z}X) is transformed into a new nucleus (^{A'}_{Z'}X'), with the release of energy.

Each of these nuclear reactions has its own unique characteristics and implications. Understanding these reactions is crucial for the development of nuclear energy and the study of nuclear physics.

#### 1.1m Nuclear Waste

Nuclear waste is a byproduct of nuclear reactions, including nuclear fission, nuclear fusion, and nuclear transmutation. It is a critical aspect of nuclear energy production and management, as it poses significant environmental and health risks if not properly managed.

Nuclear waste can be broadly categorized into two types: high-level waste and low-level waste. High-level waste is primarily spent nuclear fuel from nuclear reactors, which contains a high concentration of radioactive elements. Low-level waste, on the other hand, includes materials that have been in contact with nuclear fuel or have been used in nuclear research, but have a lower concentration of radioactive elements.

The management of nuclear waste is a complex and multifaceted issue. The primary goal is to minimize the environmental and health impacts of nuclear waste while maximizing its potential for reuse. This is achieved through a process known as nuclear fuel recycling, which involves extracting the uranium and plutonium from spent nuclear fuel and reusing them in new fuel.

The process of nuclear fuel recycling can be represented by the following equation:

$$
^{A}_{Z}X + ^{A}_{Z}X \rightarrow ^{A'}_{Z'}X' + \text{Energy}
$$

In this equation, two initial nuclei (^{A}_{Z}X) combine to form a new nucleus (^{A'}_{Z'}X'), with the release of energy. This process is known as nuclear transmutation.

Nuclear transmutation is a key component of nuclear fuel recycling, as it allows for the transformation of spent nuclear fuel into new fuel. This process not only reduces the amount of nuclear waste but also increases the efficiency of nuclear energy production.

However, nuclear transmutation also poses certain challenges. For instance, the process of nuclear transmutation can result in the formation of new radioactive elements, which can pose additional environmental and health risks. Therefore, careful management and monitoring are necessary to ensure the safe and effective use of nuclear transmutation in nuclear fuel recycling.

In conclusion, nuclear waste is a critical aspect of nuclear energy production and management. The proper management of nuclear waste, including nuclear fuel recycling and nuclear transmutation, is crucial for minimizing the environmental and health impacts of nuclear waste and maximizing the potential of nuclear energy.

#### 1.1n Nuclear Energy Production

Nuclear energy production is a complex process that involves the conversion of nuclear energy into electrical energy. This process is primarily carried out in nuclear power plants, which use nuclear reactions to generate electricity. The most common type of nuclear power plant is the light water reactor, which uses water as a coolant and moderator.

The process of nuclear energy production can be broadly categorized into three stages: nuclear fission, nuclear transmutation, and nuclear fuel recycling.

Nuclear fission is the primary nuclear reaction used in nuclear power plants. It involves the splitting of a heavy nucleus into two or more lighter nuclei, with the release of a


### Section: 1.2 Radioactive Decay

Radioactive decay is a spontaneous process in which an unstable atomic nucleus loses energy by emitting radiation in the form of particles or electromagnetic waves. This process results in the transformation of one element into another, a process known as nuclear transmutation. The study of radioactive decay is crucial for understanding the behavior of matter at the atomic level and has led to many technological advancements.

#### 1.2a Alpha Decay

Alpha decay is a type of radioactive decay in which an unstable atomic nucleus emits an alpha particle, which consists of two protons and two neutrons. This process results in a decrease in the atomic number by two and a decrease in the mass number by four. The emitted alpha particle carries a positive charge and is therefore attracted to the negative charge of the electron cloud surrounding the nucleus. This attraction can cause the alpha particle to be captured by an electron, resulting in the formation of a positive ion and a negative ion.

The probability of alpha decay occurring is dependent on the energy of the nucleus. The higher the energy of the nucleus, the greater the probability of decay. This is due to the fact that the energy of the nucleus is proportional to the square of the nuclear charge. As the nuclear charge increases, the energy of the nucleus also increases, making it more likely for the nucleus to decay.

Alpha decay is a type of spontaneous decay, meaning it occurs without the influence of external factors. This is in contrast to beta decay, which can be induced by external factors such as radiation or temperature changes. The rate of alpha decay is also dependent on the type of nucleus. Some nuclei, such as uranium and thorium, have a high probability of alpha decay, while others, such as hydrogen and helium, have a very low probability.

The energy of the emitted alpha particle is also dependent on the type of nucleus. The higher the atomic number of the nucleus, the higher the energy of the emitted alpha particle. This is due to the fact that the atomic number is directly related to the nuclear charge, and the nuclear charge is directly related to the energy of the nucleus.

Alpha decay is a crucial process in nuclear physics, as it allows for the transformation of one element into another. This process has many applications, including in the field of nuclear energy and in the development of nuclear weapons. Understanding the mechanisms and properties of alpha decay is essential for the study of nuclear physics and its applications.


#### 1.2b Beta Decay

Beta decay is another type of radioactive decay that occurs in unstable atomic nuclei. Unlike alpha decay, which involves the emission of a particle, beta decay involves the emission of an electron (or beta particle) and an electron antineutrino. This process results in a decrease in the atomic number by one and no change in the mass number. The emitted beta particle carries a negative charge and is therefore attracted to the positive charge of the nucleus. This attraction can cause the beta particle to be captured by a proton, resulting in the formation of a neutron and a positive ion.

The probability of beta decay occurring is also dependent on the energy of the nucleus. The higher the energy of the nucleus, the greater the probability of decay. This is due to the fact that the energy of the nucleus is proportional to the square of the nuclear charge. As the nuclear charge increases, the energy of the nucleus also increases, making it more likely for the nucleus to decay.

Beta decay is a type of spontaneous decay, meaning it occurs without the influence of external factors. This is in contrast to alpha decay, which can be induced by external factors such as radiation or temperature changes. The rate of beta decay is also dependent on the type of nucleus. Some nuclei, such as carbon-14 and potassium-40, have a high probability of beta decay, while others, such as hydrogen and helium, have a very low probability.

The energy of the emitted beta particle is also dependent on the type of nucleus. The higher the atomic number of the nucleus, the higher the energy of the emitted beta particle. This is due to the fact that the atomic number is directly related to the nuclear charge, and the nuclear charge is directly related to the energy of the nucleus.

Beta decay is a crucial process in nuclear physics, as it allows for the transformation of one element into another. This process has many applications, including in the field of nuclear energy and in the development of nuclear weapons. Understanding the mechanisms and properties of beta decay is essential for the study of nuclear physics and its applications.


#### 1.2c Gamma Decay

Gamma decay is a type of radioactive decay that occurs in unstable atomic nuclei. Unlike alpha and beta decay, which involve the emission of particles, gamma decay involves the emission of high-energy electromagnetic radiation known as gamma rays. This process results in no change in the atomic number or mass number of the nucleus. The emitted gamma rays carry no charge and are therefore not attracted to the positive or negative charge of the nucleus.

The probability of gamma decay occurring is also dependent on the energy of the nucleus. The higher the energy of the nucleus, the greater the probability of decay. This is due to the fact that the energy of the nucleus is proportional to the square of the nuclear charge. As the nuclear charge increases, the energy of the nucleus also increases, making it more likely for the nucleus to decay.

Gamma decay is a type of spontaneous decay, meaning it occurs without the influence of external factors. This is in contrast to alpha and beta decay, which can be induced by external factors such as radiation or temperature changes. The rate of gamma decay is also dependent on the type of nucleus. Some nuclei, such as technetium-99 and iodine-131, have a high probability of gamma decay, while others, such as hydrogen and helium, have a very low probability.

The energy of the emitted gamma rays is also dependent on the type of nucleus. The higher the atomic number of the nucleus, the higher the energy of the emitted gamma rays. This is due to the fact that the atomic number is directly related to the nuclear charge, and the nuclear charge is directly related to the energy of the nucleus.

Gamma decay is a crucial process in nuclear physics, as it allows for the transformation of one element into another. This process has many applications, including in the field of nuclear energy and in the development of nuclear weapons. Understanding the mechanisms and properties of gamma decay is essential for the study of nuclear physics and its applications.





### Subsection: 1.2b Beta Decay

Beta decay is another type of radioactive decay that occurs in unstable atomic nuclei. Unlike alpha decay, which involves the emission of a particle, beta decay involves the emission of an electron or positron. This process results in a change in the atomic number by one, while the mass number remains the same.

#### 1.2b.1 Beta Minus Decay

Beta minus decay, also known as beta emission, is a type of beta decay in which a neutron is converted into a proton, resulting in the emission of an electron and an electron antineutrino. This process is accompanied by an increase in the atomic number by one. The emitted electron carries a negative charge and is therefore attracted to the positive charge of the nucleus. This attraction can cause the electron to be captured by a proton, resulting in the formation of a negative ion and a positive ion.

The probability of beta minus decay occurring is dependent on the energy of the nucleus. The higher the energy of the nucleus, the greater the probability of decay. This is due to the fact that the energy of the nucleus is proportional to the square of the nuclear charge. As the nuclear charge increases, the energy of the nucleus also increases, making it more likely for the nucleus to decay.

Beta minus decay is a type of spontaneous decay, meaning it occurs without the influence of external factors. This is in contrast to beta plus decay, which can be induced by external factors such as radiation or temperature changes. The rate of beta minus decay is also dependent on the type of nucleus. Some nuclei, such as carbon-14 and nitrogen-14, have a high probability of beta minus decay, while others, such as hydrogen and helium, have a very low probability.

#### 1.2b.2 Beta Plus Decay

Beta plus decay, also known as positron emission, is a type of beta decay in which a proton is converted into a neutron, resulting in the emission of a positron and an electron neutrino. This process is accompanied by a decrease in the atomic number by one. The emitted positron carries a positive charge and is therefore attracted to the negative charge of the electron cloud surrounding the nucleus. This attraction can cause the positron to be captured by an electron, resulting in the formation of a positive ion and a negative ion.

The probability of beta plus decay occurring is dependent on the energy of the nucleus. The higher the energy of the nucleus, the greater the probability of decay. This is due to the fact that the energy of the nucleus is proportional to the square of the nuclear charge. As the nuclear charge increases, the energy of the nucleus also increases, making it more likely for the nucleus to decay.

Beta plus decay is a type of spontaneous decay, meaning it occurs without the influence of external factors. This is in contrast to beta minus decay, which can be induced by external factors such as radiation or temperature changes. The rate of beta plus decay is also dependent on the type of nucleus. Some nuclei, such as magnesium-23 and sodium-23, have a high probability of beta plus decay, while others, such as hydrogen and helium, have a very low probability.

#### 1.2b.3 Beta Spectrum

The beta spectrum, or distribution of energy values for the beta particles, is continuous. This means that the energy of the emitted beta particles can vary, with higher energy particles being more likely to be emitted. The total energy of the beta particles is equal to the difference in mass between the parent and daughter nuclei. This energy is carried by the beta particles and can be measured using specialized equipment.

The beta spectrum is an important tool in the study of radioactive decay. By analyzing the beta spectrum, scientists can determine the type of decay that has occurred and the energy of the emitted particles. This information can then be used to identify the parent and daughter nuclei, as well as the type of decay that has occurred.

In conclusion, beta decay is a crucial aspect of nuclear physics and plays a significant role in the study of radioactive decay. By understanding the different types of beta decay and their characteristics, scientists can gain valuable insights into the behavior of atomic nuclei and their interactions with electrons. 





### Subsection: 1.2c Gamma Decay

Gamma decay is a type of radioactive decay that occurs in unstable atomic nuclei. Unlike alpha and beta decay, which involve the emission of particles, gamma decay involves the emission of high-energy electromagnetic radiation. This process results in a change in the energy of the nucleus, but not in the atomic number or mass number.

#### 1.2c.1 Gamma Emission

Gamma emission is a type of gamma decay in which the nucleus emits a high-energy photon, resulting in a decrease in the energy of the nucleus. This process is accompanied by an increase in the atomic number by one. The emitted photon carries no charge and is therefore not affected by the electric or magnetic fields of the nucleus. This makes it difficult to study the properties of gamma radiation using traditional methods, such as deflection in an electric or magnetic field.

The probability of gamma emission occurring is dependent on the energy of the nucleus. The higher the energy of the nucleus, the greater the probability of decay. This is due to the fact that the energy of the nucleus is proportional to the square of the nuclear charge. As the nuclear charge increases, the energy of the nucleus also increases, making it more likely for the nucleus to decay.

Gamma emission is a type of spontaneous decay, meaning it occurs without the influence of external factors. This is in contrast to gamma absorption, which can be induced by external factors such as radiation or temperature changes. The rate of gamma emission is also dependent on the type of nucleus. Some nuclei, such as carbon-14 and nitrogen-14, have a high probability of gamma emission, while others, such as hydrogen and helium, have a very low probability.

#### 1.2c.2 Gamma Absorption

Gamma absorption is a type of gamma decay in which the nucleus absorbs a high-energy photon, resulting in an increase in the energy of the nucleus. This process is accompanied by a decrease in the atomic number by one. The absorbed photon carries no charge and is therefore not affected by the electric or magnetic fields of the nucleus. This makes it difficult to study the properties of gamma radiation using traditional methods, such as deflection in an electric or magnetic field.

The probability of gamma absorption occurring is dependent on the energy of the nucleus. The lower the energy of the nucleus, the greater the probability of absorption. This is due to the fact that the energy of the nucleus is proportional to the square of the nuclear charge. As the nuclear charge decreases, the energy of the nucleus also decreases, making it more likely for the nucleus to absorb a photon.

Gamma absorption is a type of induced decay, meaning it is influenced by external factors such as radiation or temperature changes. This is in contrast to gamma emission, which is a spontaneous process. The rate of gamma absorption is also dependent on the type of nucleus. Some nuclei, such as carbon-14 and nitrogen-14, have a high probability of gamma absorption, while others, such as hydrogen and helium, have a very low probability.





### Subsection: 1.2d Electron Capture

Electron capture is a type of radioactive decay that occurs in unstable atomic nuclei. Unlike alpha and beta decay, which involve the emission of particles, electron capture involves the capture of an electron by the nucleus. This process results in a change in the energy of the nucleus, but not in the atomic number or mass number.

#### 1.2d.1 Electron Capture Decay

Electron capture decay is a type of electron capture in which the nucleus captures an electron from the electron cloud surrounding the nucleus. This process results in a decrease in the energy of the nucleus, as the electron carries negative charge and therefore has a negative energy. The captured electron is typically from the K-shell, which is the innermost electron shell. This process is accompanied by an increase in the atomic number by one.

The probability of electron capture occurring is dependent on the energy of the nucleus. The higher the energy of the nucleus, the greater the probability of decay. This is due to the fact that the energy of the nucleus is proportional to the square of the nuclear charge. As the nuclear charge increases, the energy of the nucleus also increases, making it more likely for the nucleus to capture an electron.

Electron capture decay is a type of spontaneous decay, meaning it occurs without the influence of external factors. This is in contrast to positron emission, which can be induced by external factors such as radiation or temperature changes. The rate of electron capture decay is also dependent on the type of nucleus. Some nuclei, such as carbon-14 and nitrogen-14, have a high probability of electron capture decay, while others, such as hydrogen and helium, have a very low probability.

#### 1.2d.2 Positron Emission

Positron emission is a type of electron capture in which the nucleus emits a positron, which is a positively charged electron. This process results in a decrease in the energy of the nucleus, as the positron carries positive charge and therefore has a positive energy. The emitted positron is typically from the K-shell, which is the innermost electron shell. This process is accompanied by an increase in the atomic number by one.

The probability of positron emission occurring is dependent on the energy of the nucleus. The higher the energy of the nucleus, the greater the probability of decay. This is due to the fact that the energy of the nucleus is proportional to the square of the nuclear charge. As the nuclear charge increases, the energy of the nucleus also increases, making it more likely for the nucleus to emit a positron.

Positron emission is a type of induced decay, meaning it can be induced by external factors such as radiation or temperature changes. This is in contrast to electron capture decay, which is a spontaneous process. The rate of positron emission is also dependent on the type of nucleus. Some nuclei, such as carbon-14 and nitrogen-14, have a high probability of positron emission, while others, such as hydrogen and helium, have a very low probability.





### Subsection: 1.2e Internal Conversion

Internal conversion is another type of radioactive decay that occurs in unstable atomic nuclei. Unlike electron capture, which involves the capture of an electron by the nucleus, internal conversion involves the conversion of a nuclear excitation energy into an electron. This process results in a change in the energy of the nucleus, but not in the atomic number or mass number.

#### 1.2e.1 Internal Conversion Coefficient

The internal conversion coefficient, denoted by $\gamma$, is a measure of the probability of internal conversion occurring in a particular nucleus. It is defined as the ratio of the number of internal conversion decays to the total number of decays. The internal conversion coefficient is dependent on the energy of the nucleus and the type of nucleus. Some nuclei, such as carbon-14 and nitrogen-14, have a high probability of internal conversion, while others, such as hydrogen and helium, have a very low probability.

#### 1.2e.2 Internal Conversion Decay

Internal conversion decay is a type of internal conversion in which the nucleus converts a nuclear excitation energy into an electron. This process results in a decrease in the energy of the nucleus, as the electron carries negative charge and therefore has a negative energy. The converted electron is typically from the K-shell, which is the innermost electron shell. This process is accompanied by an increase in the atomic number by one.

The probability of internal conversion decay occurring is dependent on the energy of the nucleus. The higher the energy of the nucleus, the greater the probability of decay. This is due to the fact that the energy of the nucleus is proportional to the square of the nuclear charge. As the nuclear charge increases, the energy of the nucleus also increases, making it more likely for the nucleus to convert a nuclear excitation energy into an electron.

Internal conversion decay is a type of spontaneous decay, meaning it occurs without the influence of external factors. This is in contrast to electron capture, which can be induced by external factors such as radiation or temperature changes. The rate of internal conversion decay is also dependent on the type of nucleus. Some nuclei, such as carbon-14 and nitrogen-14, have a high probability of internal conversion decay, while others, such as hydrogen and helium, have a very low probability.
